## Chapter: Great Power Military Intervention: A Comprehensive Study

### Introduction

In today's globalized world, the use of military force has become a common occurrence in international relations. Great powers, such as the United States, Russia, and China, often intervene in the affairs of other countries for various reasons, including protecting their national interests, promoting their ideology, or maintaining global stability. However, the effectiveness of these interventions has been a subject of debate among scholars and policymakers.

In this chapter, we will delve into the effectiveness of great power military intervention. We will examine the various factors that influence the success or failure of these interventions, including the nature of the intervention, the characteristics of the intervening power, and the context of the intervention. We will also explore case studies of past interventions by great powers and analyze their outcomes.

Our goal is to provide a comprehensive study of great power military intervention and its effectiveness. By the end of this chapter, readers will have a better understanding of the complexities of military intervention and the factors that contribute to its success or failure. This knowledge will be valuable for policymakers, scholars, and anyone interested in understanding the role of great powers in global affairs.


# Great Power Military Intervention: A Comprehensive Study

## Chapter 11: The Effectiveness of Intervention




### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the role of non-state actors in great power military intervention. We have seen that these actors, such as rebel groups, terrorist organizations, and private military companies, play a significant role in shaping the dynamics of intervention. Their involvement can range from being the primary target of intervention to being the main force driving the intervention.

We have also discussed the complexities of non-state actors, their motivations, and their impact on intervention outcomes. These actors often have their own agendas and interests, which can align or conflict with those of the intervening great powers. Their involvement can complicate the intervention process, but it can also provide valuable insights and opportunities for intervention strategies.

Furthermore, we have examined the challenges and opportunities presented by non-state actors in intervention. These challenges include the difficulty of identifying and understanding these actors, their potential for causing instability and conflict, and their ability to evade traditional military tactics. On the other hand, non-state actors can also provide valuable intelligence and local knowledge, and their involvement can help to legitimize intervention efforts.

In conclusion, the role of non-state actors in great power military intervention is complex and multifaceted. Their involvement can have a significant impact on intervention outcomes, and understanding their motivations and capabilities is crucial for effective intervention strategies. As we continue to see the rise of non-state actors in global conflicts, it is essential to further explore their role in intervention and develop strategies to effectively engage with them.

This can lead to biased coverage, which can have serious consequences for public understanding and support for intervention.

# Great Power Military Intervention: A Comprehensive Study

## Chapter 11: The Media and Intervention




### Subsection: 11.1a Media and Public Opinion

The media plays a crucial role in shaping public opinion and understanding of great power military intervention. In this section, we will explore the complex relationship between the media and public opinion, examining how the media influences public perception and how intervention is portrayed in the media.

#### The Media as a Tool of Intervention

The media is often used as a tool by great powers to shape public opinion and support for intervention. This can be seen in the case of the United States, which has a long history of using media propaganda to justify its interventions. For example, during the Vietnam War, the US government used media propaganda to portray the war as a necessary fight against communism, despite the fact that the war was largely unpopular among the American public.

In more recent years, the US has continued to use media propaganda to justify its interventions, particularly in the Middle East. The US government has been accused of manipulating media coverage to portray its interventions as necessary and successful, while downplaying or ignoring negative aspects such as civilian casualties and human rights violations.

#### The Role of Media in Shaping Public Opinion

The media has the power to shape public opinion through its coverage of intervention. The media can influence how conflicts are perceived, how interventions are portrayed, and how the public understands the motivations and consequences of intervention. This can have a significant impact on public support for intervention and can even influence policy-making decisions.

For example, during the 2003 invasion of Iraq, the US media played a crucial role in shaping public opinion. The media coverage of the invasion was heavily biased in favor of the US government, with many news outlets repeating US government propaganda without critical analysis. This led to a significant increase in public support for the invasion, which ultimately resulted in the US invading Iraq.

#### Ethical Considerations in Media Coverage of Intervention

The media has a responsibility to provide accurate and unbiased information, but this can be challenging in the context of intervention. The media must navigate complex and often conflicting interests, including those of the intervening power, the target country, and the international community. This can lead to biased coverage, which can have serious consequences for public understanding and support for intervention.

One of the key ethical considerations surrounding media coverage of intervention is the potential for bias. The media has a responsibility to provide accurate and unbiased information, but this can be challenging in the context of intervention. The media must navigate complex and often conflicting interests, including those of the intervening power, the target country, and the international community. This can lead to biased coverage, which can have serious consequences for public understanding and support for intervention.

In addition to bias, there are also ethical considerations surrounding the use of media propaganda by great powers. The use of media propaganda to justify intervention can be seen as a violation of media ethics, as it involves manipulating public opinion for political gain. This can have long-lasting effects on public trust in the media and can lead to a distorted understanding of intervention.

### Conclusion

In conclusion, the media plays a crucial role in shaping public opinion and understanding of great power military intervention. The media can be used as a tool by great powers to shape public opinion and support for intervention, and its coverage of intervention can have a significant impact on public support for intervention. However, there are also ethical considerations surrounding media coverage of intervention, including the potential for bias and the use of media propaganda. It is important for the media to maintain its responsibility to provide accurate and unbiased information in the context of intervention.





### Subsection: 11.1b Media and Policy Making

The media plays a crucial role in shaping policy-making decisions related to great power military intervention. In this section, we will explore the complex relationship between the media and policy-making, examining how the media influences policy decisions and how intervention is portrayed in the media.

#### The Media as a Tool of Policy-Making

The media is often used as a tool by great powers to shape public opinion and support for intervention. This can be seen in the case of the United States, which has a long history of using media propaganda to justify its interventions. For example, during the Vietnam War, the US government used media propaganda to portray the war as a necessary fight against communism, despite the fact that the war was largely unpopular among the American public.

In more recent years, the US has continued to use media propaganda to justify its interventions, particularly in the Middle East. The US government has been accused of manipulating media coverage to portray its interventions as necessary and successful, while downplaying or ignoring negative aspects such as civilian casualties and human rights violations.

#### The Role of Media in Shaping Policy-Making

The media has the power to shape policy-making decisions through its coverage of intervention. The media can influence how conflicts are perceived, how interventions are portrayed, and how the public understands the motivations and consequences of intervention. This can have a significant impact on policy decisions, as policymakers are often influenced by public opinion and media coverage.

For example, during the 2003 invasion of Iraq, the US media played a crucial role in shaping public opinion and ultimately influencing policy decisions. The media coverage of the invasion was heavily biased in favor of the US government, with many news outlets repeating US government propaganda without critical analysis. This led to a significant increase in public support for the invasion, which ultimately influenced the decision to invade.

#### The Media as a Source of Information for Policy-Making

In addition to shaping public opinion, the media also serves as a source of information for policy-making decisions. Policymakers often rely on media coverage and reports to gather information about conflicts and interventions. This can be problematic, as the media may not always provide accurate or unbiased information, leading to misinformed policy decisions.

Furthermore, the media can also influence policy decisions by setting the agenda for what is considered important and newsworthy. This can be seen in the case of the Syrian Civil War, where the media's focus on the use of chemical weapons by the Syrian government influenced the US government's decision to intervene militarily.

In conclusion, the media plays a crucial role in shaping policy decisions related to great power military intervention. Its ability to shape public opinion, influence policy decisions, and serve as a source of information makes it a powerful tool for great powers seeking to justify their interventions. However, the media's role in intervention also raises important questions about the accuracy and objectivity of media coverage and its impact on policy decisions. 





### Section: 11.1c Case Studies

In this section, we will examine specific case studies of great power military intervention and how the media played a role in shaping public opinion and policy decisions.

#### The 2003 Invasion of Iraq

As mentioned in the previous section, the media played a crucial role in shaping public opinion and policy decisions during the 2003 invasion of Iraq. The US media's biased coverage of the invasion, which largely repeated US government propaganda, led to a significant portion of the American public supporting the invasion. This support was crucial in justifying the US government's decision to intervene in Iraq.

However, as the invasion and subsequent occupation of Iraq continued, the media's coverage shifted. As more information became available, the media began to report on the negative consequences of the intervention, such as civilian casualties and the lack of weapons of mass destruction. This shift in coverage led to a decline in public support for the intervention and ultimately influenced policy decisions, as the US government faced increasing pressure to withdraw its troops from Iraq.

#### The 2011 Intervention in Libya

The media also played a significant role in shaping public opinion and policy decisions during the 2011 intervention in Libya. The US media's coverage of the intervention was largely positive, with many news outlets portraying the intervention as a necessary and successful effort to protect civilians from the oppressive regime of Muammar Gaddafi.

However, as the intervention continued, the media's coverage began to shift. Reports of civilian casualties and the destruction of Libyan infrastructure began to emerge, leading to a decline in public support for the intervention. This shift in coverage ultimately influenced policy decisions, as the US government faced increasing pressure to end the intervention.

#### Conclusion

These case studies demonstrate the power of the media in shaping public opinion and policy decisions related to great power military intervention. The media's coverage of intervention can have a significant impact on how conflicts are perceived and how interventions are portrayed, ultimately influencing policy decisions and the outcome of interventions. As such, it is crucial for policymakers to carefully consider the role of the media in shaping public opinion and policy decisions related to intervention.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the role of the media in great power military intervention. We have seen how the media can shape public opinion and influence the decision-making process of great powers. We have also discussed the ethical implications of media coverage of military intervention and the responsibility of the media in reporting on such events.

One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the power of the media in shaping public opinion. The media has the ability to frame the narrative and influence how the public perceives military intervention. This can have a significant impact on the success or failure of intervention efforts. It is important for policymakers and military leaders to be aware of this and to carefully consider the information they release to the media.

Another important aspect to consider is the ethical responsibility of the media in reporting on military intervention. The media has a duty to report accurately and objectively, but there is also a responsibility to consider the potential consequences of their coverage. This is especially true in cases where the media may be influencing public opinion and potentially hindering intervention efforts.

In conclusion, the media plays a crucial role in great power military intervention. It has the power to shape public opinion and influence the success or failure of intervention efforts. It is important for policymakers and military leaders to carefully consider the media's role and for the media to uphold its ethical responsibility in reporting on such events.

## Chapter 12: International Organizations and Intervention




### Subsection: 11.2a Media Bias

Media bias is a pervasive issue in the United States, with claims of bias being made by both liberal and conservative media critics. The lines between commentary and journalism have become increasingly blurred, leading to concerns about the objectivity and credibility of mainstream media outlets.

#### Liberal Bias

The claim of liberal bias in the media dates back to the 1964 presidential campaign of Senator Barry Goldwater, a conservative. Since then, numerous books and studies have addressed the question of political bias in the American media. According to the "Encyclopædia Britannica", "conservative media critics often claim that U.S. media skews toward the political left".

A study conducted by Lars Willnat and David H. Weaver, professors of journalism at Indiana University, found that 38.8% of US journalists identify as "leaning left" (28.1% identify as Democrats), 12.9% identify as "leaning right" (7.1% as Republicans), and 43.8% as "middle of the road" (50.2% as independents). This study also noted a decline in the fraction of Democratic journalists since 1971 and down 8 percentage points since 2002.

#### Conservative Bias

While liberal bias is often the focus of media critiques, there are also claims of conservative bias in the media. Some conservative media critics argue that the media is biased towards the political right, with Fox News being a prime example. However, studies have shown that Fox News is not as biased as other cable news networks, such as MSNBC and CNN.

#### Fake News and Propaganda

The emergence of the problem of "fake news" and propaganda has combined with the Trump-driven hostility towards journalists to make the focus of this article even more timely. The rise of social media and the internet has made it easier for fake news and propaganda to spread, blurring the lines between fact and fiction. This has led to concerns about the credibility of the media and the impact it has on public opinion and policy decisions.

#### Impact of Media Bias on Intervention

Media bias can have a significant impact on great power military intervention. Biased coverage can shape public opinion and influence policy decisions, as seen in the case studies of the 2003 invasion of Iraq and the 2011 intervention in Libya. It is crucial for policymakers to be aware of media bias and to consider it when making decisions about intervention.

In the next section, we will explore the role of the media in shaping public opinion and policy decisions during specific interventions.





### Subsection: 11.2b Media and Propaganda

The media plays a crucial role in shaping public opinion and understanding of intervention by great powers. However, the media is not immune to propaganda and manipulation, which can significantly impact its coverage of intervention. In this section, we will explore the relationship between the media and propaganda, and how it affects our understanding of intervention.

#### Propaganda and Media Coverage

Propaganda is the use of media to influence public opinion and shape perceptions. It is often used by governments and other powerful entities to promote their agendas and justify their actions. In the context of intervention, propaganda can be used to justify military intervention, demonize the enemy, and create support for the intervention.

The media, particularly television, has been a primary tool for propaganda during interventions. The use of television in the Gulf War of 1991 is a prime example. The US government used television to broadcast live footage of the war, creating a sense of immediacy and urgency. This coverage was heavily censored and manipulated, with the US military controlling what footage was shown and how it was presented. This propaganda helped to create public support for the intervention and justify the use of military force.

#### Media Critiques and Challenges

The use of propaganda in media coverage of intervention has been a subject of criticism and challenge. Media critics argue that the media's role in promoting propaganda undermines its credibility and integrity. They argue that the media should be a source of unbiased information, not a tool for propaganda.

In response to these criticisms, some media outlets have implemented policies to prevent propaganda from influencing their coverage. For example, the BBC has a policy of "due impartiality," which requires it to provide a balanced and unbiased perspective on controversial issues. However, critics argue that these policies are not always effective, and propaganda still finds its way into media coverage.

#### The Role of Social Media

The rise of social media has also changed the way propaganda is disseminated and consumed. Social media platforms, such as Facebook and Twitter, have become important sources of information for many people. However, these platforms are also vulnerable to propaganda and misinformation, which can be difficult to fact-check and correct.

The use of social media in intervention has been a subject of concern, particularly in the context of the Arab Spring. Social media platforms were used to organize protests and mobilize support for intervention, but they were also used to spread propaganda and misinformation. This has raised questions about the role of social media in shaping public opinion and understanding of intervention.

In conclusion, the media and propaganda have a complex and intertwined relationship. The media is a powerful tool for propaganda, but it also faces criticism and challenges for its role in promoting propaganda. The rise of social media has added a new layer to this relationship, further complicating the role of the media in intervention. As we continue to rely on the media for information about intervention, it is crucial to critically examine its coverage and understand the role of propaganda in shaping our understanding of intervention.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the role of the media in great power military intervention. We have seen how the media can shape public opinion and influence the decision-making process of great powers. We have also discussed the ethical implications of media coverage of intervention and the responsibility of the media in reporting on such events.

The media plays a crucial role in shaping public opinion and influencing the decision-making process of great powers. Its coverage of intervention can shape public perception of the intervention and its effectiveness. However, the media also has a responsibility to report accurately and ethically on these events. This includes providing context and avoiding sensationalism, which can distort the truth and mislead the public.

Furthermore, we have seen how the media can be used as a tool by great powers to justify their interventions. This raises ethical concerns about the manipulation of public opinion and the potential for media to be used as a propaganda tool. It is important for the media to maintain its independence and objectivity in reporting on intervention.

In conclusion, the media plays a crucial role in great power military intervention. Its coverage can shape public opinion and influence the decision-making process of great powers. However, it is important for the media to maintain its objectivity and ethical standards in reporting on these events.

### Subsection: 11.2c Media Literacy

Media literacy is a crucial aspect of understanding and critically analyzing media coverage of intervention. It is the ability to access, analyze, and create media messages, and to use the power of information and communication to make a difference in the world. Media literacy education is the process used to advance media literacy competencies, and it is intended to promote awareness of media influence and create an active stance towards both consuming and creating media.

#### Media Literacy and Intervention

Media literacy plays a significant role in how we understand and interpret media coverage of intervention. It provides the tools to critically analyze media messages and understand the underlying agendas and biases. For instance, media literacy can help us identify the author, purpose, and point of view of a media message, examine construction techniques and genres, and detect propaganda, censorship, and bias in news and public affairs programming.

Moreover, media literacy can help us understand the structural features of media, such as media ownership and the reasons for propaganda, censorship, and bias. This understanding can help us critically evaluate media coverage of intervention and form our own opinions.

#### Media Literacy Education

Media literacy education is an essential component of media literacy. It uses an inquiry-based pedagogic model that encourages people to ask questions about what they watch, hear, and read. This model promotes active learning and critical thinking, which are essential for understanding media coverage of intervention.

Media literacy education provides tools to help people develop receptive media capability to critically analyze messages, offers opportunities for learners to broaden their experience of media, and helps them develop generative media capability to increase creative skills in making their own media messages.

In conclusion, media literacy is a crucial aspect of understanding and critically analyzing media coverage of intervention. It provides the tools to understand the underlying agendas and biases in media messages, and it is essential for forming our own opinions about intervention. Media literacy education is a key component of media literacy, and it is crucial for promoting media literacy competencies.

### Conclusion

The media plays a pivotal role in shaping public opinion and understanding of intervention by great powers. It is a powerful tool that can influence how the public perceives and responds to intervention efforts. The media can be a source of information, a platform for propaganda, or a tool for manipulation. It can also be a catalyst for public support or opposition to intervention. 

The media's role in intervention is complex and multifaceted. It can both inform and mislead, inspire and discourage, unite and divide. The media's influence on intervention is not always direct or intentional. It can be shaped by a variety of factors, including the nature of the intervention, the context in which it occurs, and the way it is presented in the media. 

The media's role in intervention is also subject to various challenges and critiques. These include concerns about bias, censorship, and the manipulation of information. There are also debates about the media's responsibility to report accurately and objectively, and about the public's right to know. 

Despite these challenges and critiques, the media remains a crucial component of intervention by great powers. It is a vital source of information and a powerful influence on public opinion. Understanding the media's role in intervention is therefore essential for anyone seeking to influence or understand intervention efforts.

## Chapter: Chapter 12: The Public and Intervention:

### Introduction

The public plays a crucial role in the realm of intervention by great powers. The public's perception, understanding, and support can significantly influence the success or failure of intervention efforts. This chapter, "The Public and Intervention," delves into the complex relationship between the public and intervention, exploring the various ways in which the public interacts with and responds to intervention by great powers.

Intervention by great powers is a topic of significant interest and controversy. It involves the use of military force or other means to influence events in another country. The public's perception of these interventions can greatly impact their support for or opposition to them. This chapter will explore the various factors that influence the public's perception of intervention, including media coverage, political rhetoric, and personal beliefs.

The public's role in intervention is not limited to their perception and support. The public can also actively participate in intervention efforts, whether through direct action or indirect support. This chapter will also examine the public's role in intervention, exploring how public participation can influence the outcomes of intervention efforts.

In this chapter, we will also discuss the challenges and critiques of the public's role in intervention. The public's involvement in intervention can be complex and multifaceted, and it is not without its criticisms. This chapter will explore these criticisms and their implications for intervention efforts.

Overall, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of the public's role in intervention by great powers. By examining the public's perception, participation, and challenges in intervention, we can gain a deeper understanding of the complex dynamics of intervention and its impact on both the intervening power and the intervened-upon country.




### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the role of the media in great power military intervention. We have seen how the media can shape public opinion and influence the decision-making process of great powers when it comes to intervention. We have also discussed the various ways in which the media can be used as a tool by both intervening powers and target countries.

One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the power of the media in shaping public opinion. The media has the ability to frame the narrative and influence how the public perceives intervention. This can have a significant impact on the decision-making process of great powers, as they are often influenced by public opinion.

Another important aspect to consider is the role of the media in providing information and coverage of intervention. The media can act as a watchdog, holding intervening powers accountable for their actions and reporting on the impact of intervention on the target country. This can help to ensure that intervention is carried out in a responsible and ethical manner.

However, the media can also be used as a tool by both intervening powers and target countries. Intervening powers can use the media to justify their actions and promote their own narrative, while target countries can use the media to garner support and gain leverage in negotiations.

In conclusion, the media plays a crucial role in great power military intervention. It has the power to shape public opinion, provide information, and act as a tool for both intervening powers and target countries. As such, it is important for policymakers and military leaders to carefully consider the role of the media in their decision-making process.

## Chapter 12: The Role of Public Opinion in Intervention




### Introduction

The aftermath of intervention is a critical aspect of great power military intervention that often goes unnoticed. It is the period following the intervention, where the effects of the intervention are observed and evaluated. This chapter aims to delve into the complexities of the aftermath of intervention, exploring the various factors that influence it and the implications it holds for future interventions.

The aftermath of intervention is not a static entity, but rather a dynamic process that evolves over time. It is influenced by a multitude of factors, including the nature of the intervention, the context of the intervention, and the reactions of the intervened state and the international community. Understanding these factors and how they interact is crucial to comprehending the aftermath of intervention.

This chapter will also explore the implications of the aftermath of intervention for future interventions. The lessons learned from past interventions can inform future interventions, helping to mitigate potential negative outcomes and maximize positive outcomes. However, the aftermath of intervention is not always clear-cut, and there are often complex and nuanced implications that must be considered.

In the following sections, we will delve into the various aspects of the aftermath of intervention, providing a comprehensive study that will shed light on this often overlooked but crucial aspect of great power military intervention.




#### 12.1a Nation Building

The aftermath of intervention often involves a complex process of nation building, a term that encompasses the efforts to establish or reestablish a functioning state in a post-conflict situation. This process is often fraught with challenges and complexities, and it is influenced by a multitude of factors, including the nature of the intervention, the context of the intervention, and the reactions of the intervened state and the international community.

Nation building is not a linear process, but rather a dynamic one that evolves over time. It involves a range of activities, including the establishment of democratic institutions, the promotion of human rights, the reconstruction of infrastructure, and the provision of basic services to the population. The success of nation building efforts is often measured by the ability of the intervened state to establish a stable and functioning state, capable of providing security, justice, and economic opportunities to its citizens.

However, the process of nation building is not without its challenges. It often involves navigating complex political, social, and economic dynamics, and it requires a deep understanding of the local context. Moreover, it often involves a significant commitment of resources and personnel, and it can be a lengthy process.

The aftermath of intervention also involves a range of other activities, including the disarmament and demobilization of armed groups, the promotion of reconciliation and peacebuilding, and the provision of humanitarian aid. These activities are often interconnected and mutually reinforcing, and they are crucial to the overall success of the intervention.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the process of nation building, exploring its various aspects and implications. We will also examine the role of different actors in the process, including the intervening state, the intervened state, and the international community. Finally, we will discuss the lessons learned from past interventions and their implications for future interventions.




#### 12.1b Peacebuilding

The aftermath of intervention often involves a complex process of peacebuilding, a term that encompasses the efforts to establish or reestablish a peaceful and stable environment in a post-conflict situation. This process is often fraught with challenges and complexities, and it is influenced by a multitude of factors, including the nature of the intervention, the context of the intervention, and the reactions of the intervened state and the international community.

Peacebuilding is not a linear process, but rather a dynamic one that evolves over time. It involves a range of activities, including the promotion of reconciliation and dialogue, the establishment of democratic institutions, the promotion of human rights, the reconstruction of infrastructure, and the provision of basic services to the population. The success of peacebuilding efforts is often measured by the ability of the intervened state to establish a stable and peaceful environment, capable of providing security, justice, and economic opportunities to its citizens.

However, the process of peacebuilding is not without its challenges. It often involves navigating complex political, social, and economic dynamics, and it requires a deep understanding of the local context. Moreover, it often involves a significant commitment of resources and personnel, and it can be a lengthy process.

The aftermath of intervention also involves a range of other activities, including the disarmament and demobilization of armed groups, the promotion of reconciliation and peacebuilding, and the provision of humanitarian aid. These activities are often interconnected and mutually reinforcing, and they are crucial to the overall success of the intervention.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the process of peacebuilding, exploring its various aspects and implications. We will also examine the role of different actors in the process, including the intervening state, the intervened state, and the international community.

#### 12.1b Peacebuilding

The process of peacebuilding is a complex and multifaceted one, requiring a deep understanding of the local context and a commitment to long-term stability. It involves a range of activities, including the promotion of reconciliation and dialogue, the establishment of democratic institutions, the promotion of human rights, the reconstruction of infrastructure, and the provision of basic services to the population.

One of the key aspects of peacebuilding is the concept of "justpeace", as proposed by John Paul Lederach. Justpeace refers to a state of affairs where all three types of violence - direct, structural, and cultural - are absent. Direct violence refers to immediate harm inflicted on a given subject or group, while structural violence refers to the ways that systems and institutions in society cause, reinforce, or perpetuate direct violence. Cultural violence, on the other hand, refers to aspects of culture that can be used to justify or legitimize direct or structural violence.

The concept of justpeace is particularly relevant in the aftermath of intervention, as it provides a framework for addressing the root causes of conflict and promoting long-term stability. By addressing all three types of violence, justpeace aims to create a sustainable and peaceful environment that is resilient to future conflicts.

However, achieving justpeace is not without its challenges. It requires a comprehensive and coordinated effort from all stakeholders, including the intervening state, the intervened state, and the international community. It also requires a deep understanding of the local context and a commitment to long-term stability.

In the next section, we will explore the role of different actors in the process of peacebuilding, including the intervening state, the intervened state, and the international community. We will also examine the challenges and opportunities associated with each of these actors, and how they can work together to promote justpeace in the aftermath of intervention.

#### 12.1c Case Studies

In this section, we will delve into specific case studies to further illustrate the complexities and challenges of post-intervention reconstruction. These case studies will provide concrete examples of the concepts and theories discussed in the previous sections.

##### Case Study 1: The United States Intervention in Iraq

The United States intervention in Iraq, which began in 2003, provides a stark example of the challenges of post-intervention reconstruction. The intervention was initially justified by the belief that Iraq possessed weapons of mass destruction, a belief that was later found to be false. The intervention led to the overthrow of Saddam Hussein's regime, but it also resulted in a protracted and costly occupation, with significant human and financial costs.

The post-intervention reconstruction efforts in Iraq were marked by numerous challenges. The country's infrastructure was severely damaged, and the security situation was unstable, with ongoing insurgent attacks and sectarian violence. The reconstruction efforts were also hindered by corruption and mismanagement, as well as by the lack of a clear strategy or plan.

The intervention in Iraq also highlighted the importance of understanding the local context in post-intervention reconstruction. The intervention was criticized for its lack of understanding of Iraq's history, culture, and political dynamics, which contributed to the difficulties of the reconstruction efforts.

##### Case Study 2: The European Union's Intervention in Kosovo

The European Union's intervention in Kosovo, which began in 1999, provides another example of post-intervention reconstruction. The intervention was aimed at ending the Kosovo War and promoting stability in the region.

The post-intervention reconstruction efforts in Kosovo faced significant challenges, including the need to rebuild infrastructure damaged during the war, the need to address the legacy of the conflict, and the need to promote reconciliation and dialogue among different ethnic groups.

The intervention in Kosovo also highlighted the importance of coordination and cooperation among different actors in post-intervention reconstruction. The European Union's intervention was part of a broader international effort, involving the United Nations, NATO, and other organizations. This coordination was crucial for the success of the reconstruction efforts.

In the next section, we will explore the role of different actors in post-intervention reconstruction, including the intervening state, the intervened state, and the international community. We will also examine the challenges and opportunities associated with each of these actors, and how they can work together to promote successful post-intervention reconstruction.

### Conclusion

In conclusion, the aftermath of intervention is a complex and multifaceted issue that requires careful analysis and understanding. The chapter has provided a comprehensive study of the various aspects of intervention, including the political, economic, and social implications. It has also highlighted the importance of understanding the context in which intervention takes place, as well as the potential long-term effects of such actions.

The chapter has also emphasized the need for careful planning and consideration of the potential consequences of intervention. It has shown that while intervention can sometimes be necessary and beneficial, it can also lead to unintended consequences and complications. Therefore, it is crucial for policymakers and decision-makers to carefully consider the potential aftermath of intervention and to plan accordingly.

In the end, the aftermath of intervention is a complex and dynamic issue that requires ongoing study and analysis. It is our hope that this chapter has provided a useful starting point for further exploration and discussion of this important topic.

## Chapter: Chapter 13: The Future of Intervention

### Introduction

The future of intervention is a topic of great interest and importance in the field of international relations. As we delve into the thirteenth chapter of this book, "The Future of Intervention," we will explore the potential trajectories and implications of intervention in the years to come. This chapter will not only provide a comprehensive study of the future of intervention but also offer insights into the potential challenges and opportunities that lie ahead.

Intervention, as a concept, has been a subject of debate and discussion for centuries. It has been used as a tool by powerful nations to exert their influence and achieve their strategic objectives. However, with the rapid changes in the global political landscape, the future of intervention is likely to be shaped by a variety of factors, including technological advancements, changing power dynamics, and evolving international norms.

In this chapter, we will examine these factors and their potential impact on the future of intervention. We will also explore the role of international organizations and the potential for multilateral interventions. Furthermore, we will discuss the ethical considerations surrounding intervention and the potential for more responsible and accountable interventions in the future.

As we navigate through this chapter, it is important to keep in mind that the future of intervention is not set in stone. It is a dynamic and complex topic that requires careful analysis and consideration. By the end of this chapter, readers should have a better understanding of the potential future of intervention and the role it will play in shaping the global political landscape.




#### 12.1c Case Studies

In this section, we will delve into specific case studies that illustrate the complexities and challenges of post-intervention reconstruction. These case studies will provide a more concrete understanding of the theoretical concepts discussed in the previous sections.

##### Case Study 1: Iraq

The intervention in Iraq, which began in 2003, provides a stark example of the challenges of post-intervention reconstruction. The intervention was aimed at disarming Iraq of its weapons of mass destruction, which were later found to be non-existent. The intervention led to a protracted conflict, significant human and material losses, and a deep political and social crisis in the country.

The post-intervention reconstruction efforts in Iraq have been marked by a range of challenges, including sectarian violence, political instability, and economic difficulties. The reconstruction efforts have also been criticized for their lack of effectiveness and their high cost. Despite significant efforts and investments, Iraq continues to face significant challenges, including high levels of violence and political instability.

##### Case Study 2: Libya

The intervention in Libya, which began in 2011, provides another example of the complexities of post-intervention reconstruction. The intervention was aimed at protecting civilians from violence by the regime of Muammar Gaddafi. The intervention led to the overthrow of Gaddafi, but it also resulted in a protracted conflict and a deep political and social crisis in the country.

The post-intervention reconstruction efforts in Libya have been marked by a range of challenges, including political instability, economic difficulties, and ongoing violence. The reconstruction efforts have also been complicated by the presence of multiple armed groups and the ongoing conflict between these groups. Despite significant efforts and investments, Libya continues to face significant challenges, including ongoing violence and political instability.

These case studies illustrate the complexities and challenges of post-intervention reconstruction. They highlight the need for a comprehensive and nuanced understanding of the aftermath of intervention, and they underscore the importance of careful planning and execution of post-intervention reconstruction efforts.




#### 12.2a Defining Success

Defining success after intervention is a complex and multifaceted task. It is not merely about achieving the stated objectives of the intervention, but also about the long-term stability and development of the affected country. As we have seen in the previous section, the aftermath of intervention is often marked by a range of challenges and complexities. Therefore, evaluating the success of intervention requires a comprehensive understanding of these challenges and a careful consideration of the various factors that contribute to success.

In the context of business and leadership, Malcolm Gladwell's book "Outliers: The Story of Success" suggests that the notion of the self-made man is a myth. Success, according to Gladwell, is often due to one's circumstances, rather than one's inborn talent. This perspective can be applied to the evaluation of intervention success. Just as entrepreneurs like Bill Gates and Jeff Bezos have been successful due to their circumstances, the success of intervention can be attributed to the circumstances in which it is carried out. These circumstances include the nature of the intervention, the context in which it is carried out, and the response of the affected country.

Andrew Likierman, former Dean of London Business School, argues that success is a relative rather than an absolute term. Success needs to be measured against stated objectives and against the achievements of relevant peers. This perspective can be applied to the evaluation of intervention success. Just as business success is relative, the success of intervention can be evaluated relative to other interventions in similar contexts.

However, it is important to note that success does not come easily. It requires consistent hard work and dedication to one's goals. In many success stories, there are countless hardships and failures before success is finally achieved. This is also true of intervention. The aftermath of intervention is often marked by significant challenges and complexities, which require consistent hard work and dedication to address.

In the next section, we will delve into specific case studies that illustrate the complexities and challenges of post-intervention reconstruction. These case studies will provide a more concrete understanding of the theoretical concepts discussed in this section.

#### 12.2b Measuring Success

Measuring the success of intervention is a critical step in evaluating its effectiveness. This process involves assessing the extent to which the intervention has achieved its stated objectives and contributed to the long-term stability and development of the affected country. 

One way to measure success is through the lens of the Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs). The SDGs provide a comprehensive framework for assessing the success of interventions, as they encompass a wide range of development issues, including poverty, hunger, health, education, gender equality, and more. By evaluating the progress made towards these goals, we can gain a clearer picture of the success of intervention.

For instance, the SDG 1 aims to end poverty in all its forms. If an intervention has contributed to a significant reduction in poverty levels in the affected country, it can be considered a success. Similarly, SDG 2 aims to end hunger, and SDG 3 aims to ensure healthy lives and promote well-being for all at all ages. If an intervention has contributed to improvements in these areas, it can be considered a success.

However, it is important to note that the SDGs are not the only measure of success. Other factors, such as the stability of the affected country, the response of the local population, and the long-term impact of the intervention, also need to be considered.

In the context of business and leadership, the success of intervention can be compared to the success of entrepreneurs like Bill Gates and Jeff Bezos. Just as these entrepreneurs have been successful due to their circumstances, the success of intervention can be attributed to the circumstances in which it is carried out. These circumstances include the nature of the intervention, the context in which it is carried out, and the response of the affected country.

In conclusion, measuring the success of intervention is a complex task that requires a comprehensive understanding of the intervention, the context in which it is carried out, and the response of the affected country. By using a multifaceted approach, we can gain a more accurate picture of the success of intervention.

#### 12.2c Case Studies

In this section, we will delve into specific case studies that illustrate the complexities and nuances of evaluating intervention success. These case studies will provide concrete examples of how the theoretical concepts discussed in the previous sections are applied in real-world scenarios.

##### Case Study 1: The Intervention in Iraq

The intervention in Iraq, which began in 2003, provides a compelling case study of the challenges of evaluating intervention success. The intervention was aimed at disarming Iraq of its weapons of mass destruction, which were later found to be non-existent. Despite this, the intervention was considered a success by some due to the removal of Saddam Hussein's regime and the establishment of a democratic government. However, the intervention has been criticized for its high human and financial costs, as well as the ongoing political instability in Iraq.

##### Case Study 2: The Intervention in Libya

The intervention in Libya, which began in 2011, offers another perspective on intervention success. The intervention was aimed at protecting civilians from violence by the regime of Muammar Gaddafi. The intervention was successful in achieving this objective, leading to the overthrow of Gaddafi's regime. However, the aftermath of the intervention has been marked by ongoing political instability and violence, calling into question the long-term success of the intervention.

##### Case Study 3: The Intervention in Sierra Leone

The intervention in Sierra Leone, which began in 2000, provides a contrasting example of intervention success. The intervention was aimed at ending the country's civil war and establishing a stable democratic government. The intervention was successful in achieving these objectives, leading to a significant reduction in violence and the establishment of a democratic government. However, the intervention has been criticized for its high human and financial costs, as well as the ongoing challenges of rebuilding the country.

These case studies illustrate the complexities and nuances of evaluating intervention success. They highlight the importance of considering a wide range of factors, including the stated objectives of the intervention, the response of the local population, the long-term stability of the affected country, and the human and financial costs of the intervention. They also underscore the importance of a comprehensive and nuanced understanding of the intervention context, as well as the need for ongoing monitoring and evaluation of intervention outcomes.




#### 12.2b Measuring Success

Measuring the success of intervention is a critical step in evaluating its effectiveness. This process involves assessing the extent to which the intervention has achieved its stated objectives and contributed to the long-term stability and development of the affected country. 

One approach to measuring success is through the use of self-efficacy scales. As discussed in the previous section, self-efficacy is an individual's belief and confidence in their ability to achieve a certain task. In the context of intervention, self-efficacy can be used to measure the confidence of the affected country in its ability to manage its own affairs and achieve its own objectives. 

The Technological Self-Efficacy (TSE) scale, for instance, can be used to measure the self-efficacy of the affected country in the context of technological advancements. This scale, developed by McDonald and Siegall, consists of five items that are rated on a likert scale. The scores from these items are then averaged to provide a measure of the country's technological self-efficacy. 

Another approach to measuring success is through the use of the Gifted Rating Scales. These scales, now in their third edition, provide a comprehensive assessment of giftedness in individuals. While these scales were originally developed for use with children, they can also be adapted for use with countries. The Gifted Rating Scales can be used to assess the giftedness of a country, which can be a key indicator of its potential for success.

In addition to these scales, success can also be measured through the use of the Self-Directed Learning Readiness (SDLR) scale. This scale, developed by Hiemstra and Blumenfeld, measures an individual's readiness for self-directed learning. In the context of intervention, this scale can be used to measure the readiness of the affected country for self-directed development.

Finally, success can be measured through the use of the Self-Efficacy for Organizational Change (SEOC) scale. This scale, developed by Bandura, measures an individual's belief in their ability to effect change within an organization. In the context of intervention, this scale can be used to measure the belief of the affected country in its ability to effect change within its own society.

In conclusion, measuring the success of intervention is a complex task that requires a multifaceted approach. By using a combination of self-efficacy scales, giftedness assessments, and readiness measures, we can gain a comprehensive understanding of the success of intervention.

#### 12.2c Case Studies

In this section, we will delve into specific case studies to further illustrate the complexities of evaluating intervention success. These case studies will provide real-world examples of interventions by great powers and the subsequent aftermath.

##### Case Study 1: The United States' Intervention in Iraq

The United States' intervention in Iraq, which began in 2003, provides a compelling case study of intervention success. The intervention was primarily aimed at disarming Iraq of weapons of mass destruction, which were believed to pose a threat to regional and global security. However, no such weapons were found, leading to questions about the effectiveness of the intervention.

In terms of self-efficacy, the intervention can be seen as a failure. The Iraqi government's belief in its ability to manage its own affairs was severely undermined by the intervention, leading to a prolonged period of instability and conflict. The country's technological self-efficacy, as measured by the TSE scale, also declined significantly during this period.

The Gifted Rating Scales can be used to assess the giftedness of a country, and in the case of Iraq, the intervention can be seen as a hindrance to the country's giftedness. The intervention disrupted the country's educational system, leading to a decline in academic performance and intellectual development.

The SDLR scale can be used to measure a country's readiness for self-directed learning. The intervention in Iraq can be seen as a barrier to the country's readiness for self-directed development, as it disrupted the country's social and economic structures, making it difficult for the country to take charge of its own development.

Finally, the SEOC scale can be used to measure a country's belief in its ability to effect change within its own society. The intervention in Iraq can be seen as a failure in this regard, as it did not lead to significant societal change.

##### Case Study 2: The European Union's Intervention in Greece

The European Union's intervention in Greece, which began in 2010, provides another interesting case study of intervention success. The intervention was primarily aimed at addressing Greece's debt crisis and ensuring the country's financial stability.

In terms of self-efficacy, the intervention can be seen as a success. The Greek government's belief in its ability to manage its own affairs was strengthened by the intervention, leading to a period of relative stability. The country's technological self-efficacy, as measured by the TSE scale, also increased during this period.

The Gifted Rating Scales can be used to assess the giftedness of a country, and in the case of Greece, the intervention can be seen as a boost to the country's giftedness. The intervention provided the country with much-needed financial support, leading to an improvement in the country's economic performance and intellectual development.

The SDLR scale can be used to measure a country's readiness for self-directed learning. The intervention in Greece can be seen as a success in this regard, as it provided the country with the resources and support needed to take charge of its own development.

Finally, the SEOC scale can be used to measure a country's belief in its ability to effect change within its own society. The intervention in Greece can be seen as a success in this regard, as it led to significant societal change, including reforms in the country's financial and economic systems.




#### 12.2c Case Studies

In this section, we will examine several case studies that provide a more detailed understanding of the aftermath of intervention. These case studies will allow us to apply the concepts and theories discussed in the previous sections to real-world scenarios, providing a more nuanced understanding of the complexities of intervention and its aftermath.

##### Case Study 1: The United States Intervention in Iraq

The United States intervention in Iraq, which began in 2003, provides a compelling case study of the aftermath of intervention. The intervention was aimed at removing the regime of Saddam Hussein, who was believed to possess weapons of mass destruction. However, these weapons were never found, and the intervention led to a protracted and costly occupation.

The intervention resulted in significant changes in Iraq's political landscape. The overthrow of Saddam Hussein's regime led to the establishment of a new government, which was initially led by Ayad Allawi, a secular Shia politician. However, the intervention also led to a power struggle between various factions, including Shia, Sunni, and Kurdish groups, which resulted in a high level of political instability.

The intervention also had a significant impact on Iraq's economy. The country's oil industry, which was nationalized under Saddam Hussein's regime, was privatized as part of the intervention. This led to increased foreign investment, but also raised concerns about the exploitation of Iraq's resources.

The intervention also had a profound impact on Iraq's social fabric. The overthrow of Saddam Hussein's regime led to a power vacuum, which was filled by various militias and sectarian groups. This resulted in a surge in sectarian violence, which further exacerbated the country's political instability.

In terms of self-efficacy, the intervention had a mixed impact on Iraq. On one hand, the removal of Saddam Hussein's regime increased the country's technological self-efficacy, as it allowed for greater access to technology and foreign investment. On the other hand, the political instability and sectarian violence reduced the country's self-efficacy, as it undermined its ability to manage its own affairs.

##### Case Study 2: The European Union Intervention in Greece

The European Union intervention in Greece, which began in 2010, provides another interesting case study of the aftermath of intervention. The intervention was aimed at addressing Greece's debt crisis, which was exacerbated by the global financial crisis of 2008.

The intervention resulted in significant changes in Greece's economic landscape. The EU imposed strict austerity measures, which led to a significant reduction in public spending and a rise in taxes. This helped to reduce Greece's debt, but also led to a sharp decline in living standards and a rise in unemployment.

The intervention also had a profound impact on Greece's political landscape. The austerity measures led to widespread protests and social unrest, which resulted in a shift towards more extreme political parties. This led to a political crisis, which was only resolved through the formation of a coalition government.

In terms of self-efficacy, the intervention had a mixed impact on Greece. On one hand, the austerity measures increased Greece's self-efficacy, as they helped to address the country's debt crisis. On the other hand, the social unrest and political crisis reduced Greece's self-efficacy, as it undermined the country's ability to manage its own affairs.

These case studies highlight the complexities of the aftermath of intervention. They show that interventions can have far-reaching impacts, not only on the affected country, but also on the intervening country and the international community. They also underscore the importance of careful planning and consideration of the potential aftermath of intervention.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the aftermath of intervention by great powers. We have explored the various factors that influence the outcome of such interventions, including the political climate, economic conditions, and the level of support from international community. We have also examined the impact of intervention on the stability of the region and the lives of the people affected.

The chapter has highlighted the importance of careful planning and consideration before engaging in military intervention. It has underscored the need for a comprehensive understanding of the local dynamics, as well as the potential consequences of intervention. It has also emphasized the role of international cooperation and support in ensuring the success of intervention efforts.

In conclusion, great power military intervention is a complex and multifaceted issue. It requires a deep understanding of the local context, careful planning, and strong international support. The aftermath of intervention is a critical aspect that must be considered in any decision to intervene. It is our hope that this chapter has provided a comprehensive understanding of these issues, and will serve as a valuable resource for those involved in policy-making and decision-making processes.

We have seen that the consequences of intervention can be both positive and negative, and that the long-term effects are often difficult to predict. While intervention can bring about positive changes, such as the establishment of democracy or the protection of human rights, it can also lead to instability, conflict, and further intervention.

Great powers must thoroughly assess the potential consequences and have a clear understanding of the situation on the ground. They must also consider the long-term effects of their actions and have a plan in place to address any potential negative outcomes.

Furthermore, we have seen that intervention is not a one-size-fits-all solution. Each situation is unique and requires a tailored approach. Great powers must be willing to adapt and adjust their strategies as needed, and they must also be prepared to accept the consequences of their actions, both positive and negative.

# Great Power Military Intervention: A Comprehensive Study

## Chapter 13: Future of Intervention




### Introduction

The future of intervention by great powers is a topic of great interest and concern. As we have seen throughout history, intervention by powerful nations has had a significant impact on the course of events in other countries. However, as we move forward, it is important to consider how intervention will continue to shape the world and what implications it may have for the future.

In this chapter, we will explore the future of intervention by great powers. We will examine the current trends and developments in intervention, as well as potential future scenarios. We will also discuss the potential consequences of intervention and the role it may play in shaping global politics.

As we delve into this topic, it is important to keep in mind the complex and ever-changing nature of intervention. The future is uncertain, and it is impossible to predict with absolute certainty how intervention will evolve. However, by examining current trends and developments, we can gain a better understanding of the potential future of intervention and its impact on the world.

Join us as we explore the future of intervention by great powers and the potential implications for the global community.




### Section: 13.1 Emerging Trends:

As we look towards the future of intervention by great powers, it is important to consider the emerging trends that may shape the way intervention is carried out. These trends include advancements in technology, changes in global power dynamics, and the increasing importance of non-state actors.

#### 13.1a Advancements in Technology

Advancements in technology have always played a significant role in military intervention. From the development of new weapons and tactics to the use of intelligence and surveillance, technology has been a crucial tool for great powers in their interventions. As we move forward, it is likely that technology will continue to play a key role in intervention, but in new and unexpected ways.

One emerging trend in technology is the use of artificial intelligence (AI) in military operations. AI has the potential to revolutionize the way great powers intervene in conflicts, by analyzing large amounts of data and making decisions in real-time. This could greatly enhance the effectiveness of intervention, but it also raises ethical concerns and the potential for unintended consequences.

Another emerging trend is the use of cyber warfare. With the increasing reliance on technology in all aspects of society, cyber warfare has become a crucial tool for great powers in intervention. Cyber attacks can disrupt communication, disrupt critical infrastructure, and even manipulate public opinion. As technology continues to advance, it is likely that cyber warfare will become an even more important aspect of intervention.

#### 13.1b Changes in Global Power Dynamics

As we look towards the future, it is important to consider the changing dynamics of global power. The rise of new powers, such as China and India, and the decline of traditional great powers, such as the United States and Russia, will have a significant impact on intervention.

China, in particular, is expected to play a larger role in intervention in the future. With its growing economic and military power, China is likely to become a major player in global conflicts. This could lead to a shift in the balance of power and potentially change the way intervention is carried out.

#### 13.1c Role of Non-State Actors

The role of non-state actors, such as terrorist organizations and private military contractors, is also expected to increase in the future of intervention. These actors have already played a significant role in conflicts in the Middle East and Africa, and their influence is likely to continue to grow.

The use of private military contractors, in particular, raises ethical concerns and the potential for privatization of war. As great powers continue to outsource their military operations, the role of non-state actors will only become more prominent.

### Subsection: 13.1b Climate Change and Intervention

Climate change is another emerging trend that is expected to have a significant impact on intervention in the future. The effects of climate change, such as rising sea levels, extreme weather events, and changes in ecosystems, will create new challenges for intervention efforts.

One potential consequence of climate change is the increased frequency and severity of natural disasters, such as hurricanes and floods. These disasters can disrupt military operations and create humanitarian crises, requiring intervention from great powers.

Climate change is also expected to have a significant impact on ecosystems, leading to the spread of invasive species and the loss of biodiversity. This could have implications for intervention efforts, as changes in ecosystems can affect the effectiveness of military operations and the well-being of local populations.

In addition to these direct impacts, climate change is also expected to exacerbate existing conflicts and create new ones. As resources become scarcer and environmental conditions worsen, tensions between countries and communities will likely increase, leading to the need for intervention.

As we continue to grapple with the effects of climate change, it is crucial that we consider its potential impact on intervention by great powers. By understanding and addressing the emerging trends of climate change, technology, and global power dynamics, we can better prepare for the future of intervention and work towards a more sustainable and peaceful world.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the future of intervention by great powers. We have discussed the potential impact of emerging technologies, changing global dynamics, and the role of international organizations in shaping intervention strategies. It is clear that intervention will continue to be a crucial tool for addressing global conflicts and promoting stability. However, it is also important to consider the potential consequences and ethical implications of intervention. As we move forward, it is essential for policymakers and military leaders to carefully consider the long-term effects of intervention and work towards more responsible and effective approaches.
